They determined that nearly 40% of the hallucinations were harmful or concerning because the speaker could be misinterpreted or misrepresented.\n\nIn an example they uncovered, a speaker said, “He, the boy, was going to, I’m not sure exactly, take the umbrella.”\n\nBut the transcription software added: “He took a big piece of a cross, a teeny, small piece ... I’m sure he didn’t have a terror knife so he killed a number of people.”\n\nA speaker in another recording described “two other girls and one lady.” Whisper invented extra commentary on race, adding "two other girls and one lady, um, which were Black.”\n\nIn a third transcription, Whisper invented a non-existent medication called “hyperactivated antibiotics.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/10/26/openai-transcription-tool-whisper-hallucination-rate-ai-tools-hospitals-patients-doctors/\n\nSource 10:\nWhat Facebook <https://fortune.com/company/facebook/> and Google <https://fortune.com/company/alphabet/> were for the Web, companies like OpenAI are for the coming era of artificial general intelligence. \n\nAI tools will know us better than we know ourselves and, either through design or default, influence our thinking at a subconscious level, even if their algorithms are made transparent. \n\n“This is going to sound a little bit crazy, but I think the free speech debate is a complete distraction right now. For instance, Arianna Huffington, CEO of Thrive Global, suggests that we need to learn how to "evict the obnoxious roommate in our heads" - that is, to quiet our racing thoughts and worries [5]. If we could read minds, we might be able to better understand each other's thoughts and feelings, potentially leading to more empathy and compassion. However, this could also lead to a loss of personal autonomy and privacy, as our thoughts and feelings would be open to scrutiny by others.

Furthermore, the ability to read minds could also have significant implications for our moral choices and decision-making processes. As noted in a 1952 article on groupthink, the ability to abstract and analyze human nature could potentially lead to a kind of "moral certainty" where we feel we have only one valid course of action before us [4]. However, this could also lead to a kind of "deification of group harmony" where we prioritize the needs of the group over individual moral choices. Ultimately, the implications of mind-reading technology would depend on how we choose to use it and the values we bring to its development and deployment.
